Given the conflicting and non-comparable characteristics of the approximated Pareto sets, it is advisable to employ a range of metrics for a comprehensive quality evaluation. In this context, this study employs three widely recognized performance indicators: Maximum Diversity (MD), Spacing Metric (SP), and the Rate of Pareto Solutions (NOS), as detailed by Gharaei and Jolai (2018) . These metrics are concisely summarized in Table 5  . Metaheuristic algorithms' performance is highly dependent on their parameters, so they should be tuned. The Taguchi method and the RSM are both widely used for tuning. Taguchi's method (Taguchi, 1986 ) uses discrete levels to tune algorithms, whereas the RSM (Box and Wilson, 1992 ) uses continuous levels. The input parameters for metaheuristics are defined as factors, with each factor (x i    ) measured at two levels, coded from − 1    to 1   , depending on what the range is (i.e., an interval range of each factor). Therefore, the independent variables (z i    ) regarding the factors are:(53) z i  = x i  − ( x h  + x l   )  / 2  ( x h  − x l   )  / 2   , i = { 1 , 2 , . . . , K  }      where K    corresponds to the number of variables. The following polynomial response surface function (y   ) is used to compute the response of the set of independent variables:(54) y = β 0  + ∑ j = 1  k  β j  z j  + ∑ j = 1  k  ∑ i < j  k  β i j   z i  z j  + ∑ j = 1  k  β j j   z j j  2  + ε     where β 0    , β j    , β i j      and β j j      are the constant of the linear coefficient, the interaction coefficient (β i j     ), and the quadratic coefficient (β j j     ), respectively. For each factor, the span of parameters must be specified, so initial ranges of parameters are obtained from literature, for instance from (Gharib et al., 2022 ; Seydanlou et al., 2022 ). The algorithms should be fine-tuned using all of the test problems, and the average is used to determine the recommended parameters for algorithms.  5.4.2 Computation results In this section, we begin our analysis by assessing the capability of metaheuristic algorithms to find solutions that are closely aligned with the optimal ones for Problem #4. This evaluation involves a comparison against benchmarks set by the results of a commercial solver. Subsequently, we leverage the predefined performance metrics to conduct an in-depth comparative analysis, comparing the metaheuristic algorithms against one another across a series of increasingly complex problem instances. The Hypervolume (HV) metric (Fig. 9  ) is utilized for this comparison, including against the commercial solver, and its results are illustrated in Fig. 10  . To ensure a thorough comparison, we undertake multiple iterations—60, to be precise—to compile a comprehensive set of solution sets. In each iteration, we calculate the Hypervolume for the solutions produced by both the commercial solver and the metaheuristic algorithm. These calculations enable us to track the percent of the absolute differences in Hypervolume between the two methodologies throughout the 60 iterations. The aggregated data from these iterations is then presented in a box plot, providing a succinct and clear visual representation of the performance discrepancies between the methods, as shown in Fig. 10 . The outcomes indicate that across 60 repetitions, a significant majority of the cases yielded an absolute gap of zero between the implemented algorithm and the commercial solver. In instances where a gap was present, it was found to be minimal, underscoring the robust performance of the algorithms, particularly MOPSO and MOEA/D. This is especially noteworthy in the context of this test problem, where the discrepancy between optimal solutions was relatively minimal. Upon a thorough examination of the performance metrics outlined in Table 5 , we can determine the metaheuristic algorithm that exhibits the most exceptional performance. The values for each metric are presented in Table 6  . Notably, when compared to other metaheuristic algorithms, MOPSO demonstrates substantial performance advantages. In all instances, MOPSO consistently maintains its high-level performance. Detailed discussions of the results for each metric are provided in Figs. 11–13    . In terms of the average number of Pareto solutions, MOPSO consistently exhibited superior performance compared to other methods across all replications. The exception was Problem #04, where MOEA/D outperformed MOPSO. However, MOEA/D faced challenges in delivering desirable results for Problems #05, #06, and #07. Remarkably, on average, MOEA/D was able to identify 34 solutions for Problem #04 that remained non-dominated by other algorithms. Following MOEA/D, MOPSO came closest with 26 solutions. In Problem #05, MOPSO's performance stood out with an average of 48 non-dominated solutions, which was at least double the count achieved by the second-best performing algorithm, NSGA-II. For Problems #06 and #07, the algorithms demonstrated relatively similar performance. SPEA2 consistently had the lowest performance, identifying an average of 14.129 solutions. It's worth noting that PESA-II closely mirrored this performance with 14.592 solutions. In contrast, MOPSO consistently excelled, with over 80% of its solutions—averaging 41.813—being non-dominated solutions. Fig. 11  provides a comprehensive overview of the algorithms' performance based on the average number of non-dominated solutions. Utilizing the boundary solutions acquired, Fig. 12  visually depicts the distances between these solutions. Interestingly, no discernible specific pattern emerges across the various algorithms. Notably, PESA-II consistently exhibited the weakest performance across all instances, while MOPSO consistently showcased the most favorable performance. The performance of MOPSO and NSGA-II closely paralleled each other, with instances where NSGA-II even outperformed MOPSO, notably in Problems #05 and #06. Fig. 13  provides an illustration of the distribution of non-dominant solutions along the Pareto front. Notably, MOPSO consistently outperformed other metaheuristic algorithms in every instance, achieving an average superiority of 0.057. In Problems #05, #06, and #07, NSGA-II demonstrated the best performance following MOPSO, with corresponding metrics of 0.028, 0.039, and 0.032. It's worth highlighting that, apart from MOPSO and NSGA-II, the remaining algorithms consistently delivered satisfactory performance based on this metric, showcasing their competence in various problem scenarios.   5.5 Sensitivity analyses This subsection presents sensitivity analyses to illustrate the model's efficiency. It is important to note that the entire sensitivity analysis process is conducted on a particular Pareto-optimal solution. The analysis begins by examining the critical parameter of distance between nodes, an essential factor in routing problems. Fig. 14   demonstrates the impact of the distance between node i    and j    (denoted as d i j  i j     ) on various variables. An increase in d i j  i j      leads to a rise in the first objective function (Z 1    ) due to the increased variable costs associated with longer distances between nodes. On the other hand, the second objective function (Z 2    ) tends to decrease, as greater distances typically result in higher fuel consumption, thereby reducing the total remaining fuel in routing vehicles. The third objective function (Z 3    ) remains relatively unaffected. Furthermore, a longer distance is likely to result in a longer average route time (R T   ). The analysis then shifts to the impact of the product return rate. Fig. 15   explores how the product return rate (r R  h s     ) affects the model. An increase in r R  h s      typically requires setting up more repair and recycling centers (represented as S R    and S E   ), leading to a significant increase in the number of returned products processed by these facilities. As a result, the first objective function (Z 1    ) is likely to increase due to higher costs associated with establishing and transferring to these new centers. The second objective function (Z 2    ) remains unaffected by this change. However, the third objective function (Z 3    ) sees an increase, as the establishment of more centers correlates with the creation of additional job opportunities. The subsequent phase of the analysis examines the impact of the maximum route time, a factor of significant importance in the model, especially in terms of the operational decision-making timeframe. Fig. 16   illustrates the consequences of adjusting the maximum allowable time for routes (T m a x   ) on decision-making. A reduction in T m a x    leads to a noticeable decrease in the average route time (R T   ). This, in turn, necessitates an increase in the number of routing vehicles (Y   ) used to fulfill the demands. This decrease in route time is likely to cause an increase in the first objective function (Z 1    ). In a similar vein, the second objective function (Z 2    ) is expected to rise due to the shorter travel times and the reduced number of stops per routing vehicle. Moreover, employing more vehicles correlates with more job creation, thereby enhancing the third objective function (Z 3    ). The evaluation now focuses on assessing the impact of the maximum allowed vehicles of depots. This parameter stands as a sensitive parameter, exerting influence on various variables. Fig. 17   demonstrates the effect of maximum allowed number of allowed vehicles of each DC/CC (A V   ) on some variables. An increase in A V    leads to a rise in the number of employed routing vehicles (i.e., Y). Interestingly, an inverse relationship is noticeable; as each DC/CC is capable of hiring more routing vehicles, the number of opened DC/CCs (i.e., SD) decreases. Consequently, the first objective function (i.e., Z 1    ) experiences a downward trajectory. Meanwhile, the second objective function (i.e., Z 2    ) shows marginal alterations. The reduction in the necessity for opened DC/CCs contributes to a decrease in the third objective function (i.e., Z 3    ) and created career opportunities. Next, the effect of created job opportunities by the establishment of a repair centers (J o e  e h     ) are analyzed and illustrated in Fig. 18  . As can be seen, by increasing J o e  e h     , total created job opportunities will rise and the third objective function (i.e., Z 3    ) will be enhanced, new repair centers will establish which are even more than the needs. Number of repairs centers (i.e., S E   ) has increased while the number of recycling centers (i.e., S R   ) is constant. The establishment of new repair centers has caused an increase in total costs and the first objective function (i.e., Z 1    ). The second objective function (i.e., Z 2    ) remains unchanged. Finally, the relation between scenarios and objective functions, as sustainability goals, are analyzed. The scenarios, generated as outputs of the backward scenario reduction method, are essential components in our model as they approximate continuous probability distributions with discrete scenarios. In this analysis, we systematically varied the generated scenarios to understand their influence on the model outcomes. The results of this sensitivity analysis are presented in Fig. 19  , providing valuable insights into how changes in scenarios impact the performance of our proposed model. As can be seen in Fig. 19 , two scenarios are generated using the backward scenario reduction method. In the first scenario, the total demand remains relatively stable. However, in the second scenario, the product rate of return is slightly higher than in the first scenario. Alongside alterations in distribution and collection planning, there are more items to collect in the second scenario. This increase in the number of returned products, coupled with changes in routing, leads to higher costs, fuel consumption, and an increased demand for recycling and repair centers. It is noteworthy that changes in distribution and collection planning, influenced by variations in demand and product rate of return, lead to more rapid alterations in the environmental objective function (i.e., Z 2    ). Subsequently, the social objective function (i.e., Z 3    ) experiences more pronounced changes compared to the economic objective function (i.e., Z 1  )   . Table 7   examines the relationship between the percentage of recyclable products (r R C  h    ) and the percentage of repairable products (r R P  h    ). In the problem's input parameters, equal emphasis is placed on both repairing and recycling returned products. This implies that the costs and job creation associated with establishing either a repair or a recycling center are roughly equivalent. A balance between recyclable and repairable products leads to a reduction in overall system costs. However, if either recyclables or repairables dominate, it results in an increase in the total cost of the system. The planning for distribution is influenced by factors such as demand (denoted as d d  c h s     ) and distance (d i j  i j     ). This analysis focuses on understanding how demand impacts the distribution model. In Table 8  , adjustments in d d  c h s      result in variations in the distribution planning. As the overall demand escalates, there is an increase in the network's total costs and the necessity for more jobs. The growth in demand leads to the utilization of vehicles with greater capacity, which, in turn, have higher fuel consumption. Consequently, this escalates costs and reduces the remaining fuel in vehicles. While other parameters remain constant, the routing itself remains unchanged, but there are alterations in the loaded quantity within the vehicle and the remaining fuel at different nodes. A significant shift in demand can lead to changes in the routing plan and an increase in costs. When demand varies widely, it leads to increased fuel consumption and the need for more vehicles, even if the total demand is lower. Conversely, if demand variations are minimal, even with high overall demand, the management and routing of the network become more manageable. The fuel consumption of routing vehicles is influenced by the type of vehicle, the distance traveled, and the traveling speed. The traveling speed is indicative of road congestion. During periods of high congestion, meeting the maximum time requirements for serving nodes becomes challenging. Additionally, traffic congestion leads to increased fuel consumption, necessitating the use of vehicles with better fuel efficiency. However, this shift towards more fuel-efficient vehicles can result in higher operational costs. Table 9   explores the relationship between the average speed of vehicles between nodes (denoted as S i j  i j     ) and other related variables. It is evident that when roads are congested and average speeds are low, the use of more expensive, fuel-efficient vehicles becomes necessary. This leads to increased costs and the creation of more jobs in the network.   6 Discussion & managerial insight In this study, we developed a three-echelon SCLSC network, integrating crucial decisions about location, allocation, and routing. This versatile model is adaptable to various industries and product types, encompassing both the distribution of products and the concurrent collection of returns. To maintain the model's universal applicability, we categorized returned products into two groups: recyclable and repairable. Recyclable products are processed into new items, while repairable ones are refurbished for resale in secondary markets. Our model addresses all key strategic, tactical, and operational decisions within the network, including site selection, resource allocation, supplier evaluation, transportation, fleet sizing, and routing, through a dual-stage framework focused on three core objectives of sustainable networks. Incorporating simultaneous pick-up and delivery with green VRP in these networks not only reduces costs but also significantly contributes to environmental preservation. Our goal now is to offer insights and guidance to managers and decision-makers. By adopting these recommendations, they can not only make more efficient and cohesive decisions but also significantly advance toward sustainability objectives and fully embrace the principles of the circular economy.• Understanding the impact of distances between nodes is crucial for cost-effective and environmentally friendly operations. Managers should prioritize the deployment of fuel-efficient and eco-friendly vehicles for longer routes. Although this may initially increase expenses, the long-term benefits in fuel savings and reduced environmental footprint are substantial. To offset the longer travel times, logistics strategies such as extended route times and adjusted work schedules can be implemented to maintain efficiency without incurring significant additional costs. Incorporating a GPS-based route optimization system that dynamically adapts to traffic conditions and distances ensures the most efficient routes are always utilized.  • In SSC, the rate of product return is a pivotal factor. Managers can view increased return rates as an opportunity to enhance the circular economy, even though it may raise system costs. Increased product returns offer the potential for job creation and promote consumer participation in sustainability. Balancing the establishment costs of recovery centers against social and environmental benefits requires careful financial planning. Initiatives like consumer incentive programs for product returns, coupled with investment in automated systems at recycling and repair centers, can efficiently handle increased volumes.  • Vehicle allocation decisions are crucial, with more vehicles at each depot potentially reducing costs but also impacting job opportunities. Managers must judiciously balance cost efficiency with social responsibilities like job creation. Diversifying the vehicle fleet and forming local job creation partnerships can offset potential job reductions, harmonizing cost savings with employment opportunities.  • The variability in demand significantly affects supply chain dynamics. In high-demand scenarios, having adequate center and vehicle capacity, and scalable workforce is essential. In situations of pronounced demand fluctuations, adopting flexible and eco-conscious routing strategies is crucial. Utilizing advanced demand forecasting tools powered by AI and machine learning can help predict demand changes and optimize resource allocation.  • Traffic congestion directly influences fuel consumption and routing efficiency. Scheduling deliveries during less congested times and exploring alternative, less congested routes, even if longer, can result in fuel and time savings. Employing traffic prediction technologies and alternative route planning software can optimize routing decisions in real-time and circumvent peak traffic hours.  • Managing uncertainties, like fluctuating demand and product return rates, necessitates a flexible and dynamic management approach. Focusing on adaptable strategies that effectively harmonize economic, environmental, and social objectives is essential. Establishing a team dedicated to monitoring market trends and customer behaviors enables swift adaptation of supply chain strategies to emerging conditions.     7 Conclusion This study introduced an integrated location-allocation-routing problem within a three-echelon SCLSC network, encompassing PCs, DC/CCs, DPs, recycling centers, repair centers, and secondary markets. Two types of transportation were considered: the first involved direct transfer of vehicles between PCs and DC/CCs in the direct flow and between DC/CCs and repair or recycling centers in the reverse flow. The second involved routing distribution vehicles between DC/CCs and DPs to simultaneously deliver products and collect returned items. Green routing was implemented, taking into account vehicle speed and fuel consumption between nodes. The first objective function sought to minimize total costs, the second aimed to maximize the remaining fuel in each vehicle to reduce CO2 emissions, and the third sought to augment job opportunities locally throughout the network. The model promoted circular economy goals in the supply chain by incorporating product returns. It was designed to integrate strategic, tactical, and operational decisions within the supply chain network. Considering real-world conditions, demand and product return rate uncertainties were modeled using stochastic programming. To generate suitable scenarios, a heuristic backward scenario reduction method was employed. The model's performance was validated by solving four small-sized instances in GAMS software using an improved ε-constraint method, AUGMECON2. Additionally, five metaheuristic algorithms—NSGA-II, MOPSO, SPEA2, MOEA/D, and PESA-II—were applied to larger problems. To confirm the model's performance and validity, sensitivity analyses and managerial insights for decision-makers were provided. In conclusion, the outcomes of the proposed model are summarized as follows.• The MOPSO demonstrated superior performance across various metrics when compared to alternative algorithms. Notably, it excelled in terms of the rate of Pareto solutions, maximum diversity metric, and spacing metrics.  • Increased investment holds the potential for substantial enhancements in environmental and social standards. Allocating additional funds and establishing more centers not only facilitates job creation but also fosters long-term individual development and employment. Furthermore, heightened investment enables the adoption of environmentally friendly and fuel-efficient vehicles, contributing to a significant reduction in environmental impacts.  • The products rate of return emerges as a crucial parameter, playing a pivotal role in advancing the principles of circular economy and sustainability. Additionally, it serves as a catalyst for job creation.    This study acknowledges certain limitations and suggests areas for improvement to be addressed in future research.• The current model did not include disruption and resilience strategies. Future research could explore integrating these strategies to enhance the model's ability to handle unexpected events. This investigation is crucial for understanding the robustness and adaptability of the proposed network, contributing valuable knowledge for effective risk mitigation.  • The proposed model excluded split delivery considerations. Future studies should examine the impact of split delivery on the network's performance, particularly in terms of social and environmental metrics. This exploration will offer deeper insights into the effectiveness and sustainability implications of split deliveries in supply chain networks.  • In the developed model, separate refuel stations were not included, despite being a common feature in related literature. For enhanced realism, future iterations of the model should consider incorporating the option for vehicles to refuel during trips at these stations. This addition could provide a more practical and applicable framework for supply chain management.  • Incorporating inventory decisions into the current model represents a significant avenue for future research. This involves developing more comprehensive models that seamlessly integrate inventory management with other key aspects of supply chain decisions. Such integration would include a detailed focus on varying inventory levels, effective replenishment strategies, and accurate demand forecasting mechanisms. Further investigation is needed to understand how different inventory policies influence the overall performance and resilience of the supply chain, particularly in response to fluctuating market demands and supply chain disruptions. Additionally, exploring advanced inventory strategies, such as Vendor-Managed Inventory (VMI) and Just-In-Time (JIT) delivery, within the context of the proposed model is crucial.    Addressing these limitations will not only strengthen the model but also extend its applicability and relevance to real-world supply chain challenges.
